Nasafi's Method in Refuting Eternalists
Nasafi states his methodology and intention to prove the world's creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Nasafi's approach to refuting the eternalist arguments by establishing proof of the world's temporal origination.

Core Idea
Refutation begins with proof of creation, not with attacking details.

After presenting the eternalist views, Imam Nasafi explains his methodology: he will not delve into every secondary argument but will focus on the main ones. He then announces his plan to first establish proof that the entire world is temporally originated, after which he will refute the eternalist claims.

Guided Path
1
Locate the main arguments
Read the sentence 'هذه عمدة أقاويل هؤلاء' and understand that the author considers what he has mentioned as the core arguments.
He deliberately omits secondary branches.
2
Notice the author's selectivity
The author refrains from mentioning peripheral arguments because they are outside the scope of his book.
This shows his focused approach.
3
Understand the plan
The author states he will first prove that the world in all its parts is temporally originated (حادث), and then show the corruption of the eternalist arguments.
He prioritizes proof of creation over direct refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تبصرة الأدلة لأبي المعين النسفي ج
الناس نسبة هذا القول إليه وقالوا: إنه كان يقول بحدوث العالم وثبوت الصانع القديم، والمذهب المشهور عن جالينوس التوقف في القول بحدوث العالم وقدمه، وثبوت الصانع وعدمه، وفي ثبوت المعاد ونفيه. ومنهم من زعم أن أصل العالم الطبائع المركبة، وهي النار والماء والأرض والهواء ويسمونها العناصر، وقد يسمونها الإسطقسات. وشبهة هؤلاء كلهم أن وجود الشيء لا من شيء محال وكل صانع يصنع في محل إلا أن يوجد المحل أصلا.
هذه عمدة أقاويل هؤلاء، ولهم أقاويل مختلفة جارية مجرى الفروع لهذه فأعرضنا عن ذكرها لخروجها عما هو شرط كتابنا هذا، ونبين فساد هذه الأقاويل بعد إقامة الدليل على كون العالم بجميع أقسامه محدثا إن شاء الله تعالى.
The people attributed this saying to him and said: He used to affirm the temporal origination of the world and the existence of the eternal Maker. The famous doctrine attributed to Galen is suspension of judgment regarding the temporal origination and eternity of the world, the existence and non-existence of the Maker, and the affirmation and negation of the resurrection. And among them are those who claim that the origin of the world is the composite natures, which are fire, water, earth, and air; they call them the elements, and they sometimes call them the stoicheia. The doubt of all these is that the existence of a thing from nothing is impossible, and every maker makes in a substrate, unless the substrate exists originally. These are the main points of their sayings. They have various other sayings that are like branches of these, so we refrained from mentioning them because they are outside what is required for this book of ours. We will show the corruption of these sayings after establishing the proof that the world in all its parts is temporally originated, God willing.
Source pages p. 203

This passage is a survey of the opinions of those who claim the world or parts of it are eternal. The author first mentions an unnamed person (perhaps Galen) to whom people attribute the belief in the world's temporality and the existence of an eternal Maker, though the famous view of Galen is suspension of judgment on these matters. The author states that these are the main arguments, and he will not mention their secondary branches. He then declares his intention to refute these views after establishing proof that the entire world is temporally originated (حادث). This sets the stage for the subsequent proof.

The Objection of a Single Substance and Its Refutation
Al-Nasafi responds to the objection about a single substance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Nasafi responds to the objection that a single substance could be free from accidents, and why this does not undermine the proof of the temporal origination of bodies.

Core Idea
A single substance is irrelevant; the real argument requires two substances and time.

This passage presents a key objection from materialists (dahriyya) who argue that if God created only one substance, it would be free from motion, rest, conjunction, and separation. Al-Nasafi replies that this objection is actually turned against them: the proof of temporality relies on multiple substances and their necessary relation to accidents. He clarifies that he admits the possibility of a single substance being accident-free, but that is not the point at issue.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection and its basis
Read the objector's claim: a single substance lacks place and other substances, so it has no motion/rest or conjunction/separation.
This is the dahri's objection.
2
Recognize al-Nasafi's counter: this objection is ours
He says this objection is from us against those who deny temporal origination. It is a tool against them, not a problem for us.
3
Understand the shift to two substances and time
The real argument uses pairs: two substances cannot avoid conjunction/separation; a body in place over two moments cannot avoid motion/rest.
This is the core proof.
4
See the concession: a single substance can be free
He admits that a single hypothetical substance could be free, but the actual world has many substances.
The concession shows the objection is irrelevant.
5
Grasp the conclusion: the discussion revolves around actual existents
The impossibility of being free from accidents applies to the observable world, proving its temporality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : لو أوجد الله تعالى أول ما أوجد جوهرا واحدا لكان خاليا عن الحركة والسكون لانعدام المكان وكذا عن الاجتماع والافتراق لانعدام ما يكون بجنبه أو لا بجنبه قلنا هذا الإلزام منا على من أنكر حدوث الأجسام وادعى قدم جميع أجزاء العالم، فقلنا: إن وجود الجوهرين لا يخلو من الاجتماع والافتراق، وكذا وجود المكان والمتمكن زمانين لا يخلو عن الحركة أو السكون ، لنلزم الدهرى استحالة خلو أجرام العالم وأجسامه عن الأعراض، فأما إذا سلم لنا حدوثها كلها فلم يبق بنا حاجة إلى إثبات ذلك بواسطة إثبات استحالة تعريها عن الأعراض، على أنا ندعي استحالة تعري الجوهرين عن الاجتماع والافتراق والاستحالة تعري المتمكن في المكان في حالة البقاء عن الحركة والسكون ليظهر أن أعيان العالم ما نشاهد يمتنع خلوها عن الأعراض فيستحيل قدمها إذ قدم ما لا يسبق الحادث محال وذلك كما ادعينا، فأما نحن فنسلم أن الله تعالى لو خلق جوهرا واحدا لكان خاليا عن الاجتماع والافتراق والحركة والسكون فأما خلو جوهرين لو خلقهما الله تعالى عن الاجتماع والافتراق وخلو المكان والمتمكن في حالة البقاء عن الحركة والسكون فمحال، والكلام وقع في هذا
If it is said: If God most-high were to create from the first a single substance, it would be free from motion and rest due to the non-existence of place, and likewise free from conjunction and separation due to the non-existence of something adjacent or not adjacent to it. We say: This objection is from us against those who deny the temporal origination of bodies and claim the pre-eternity of all parts of the world. So we say: The existence of two substances is not free from conjunction and separation, and likewise the existence of place and that which occupies a place in two times is not free from motion or rest, in order to compel the materialist (dahri) regarding the impossibility of the bodies of the world being free from accidents. As for if they admit to us the temporal origination of all of them, we have no need to prove that by means of proving the impossibility of their being divested of accidents. Moreover, we claim the impossibility of two substances being free from conjunction and separation, and the impossibility of that which occupies a place being free from motion and rest in the state of remaining, so that it becomes clear that the individual entities of the world that we observe cannot be free from accidents, thus their pre-eternity is impossible, for the pre-eternity of that which is not preceded by the originated is impossible, and that is as we claim. As for us, we admit that if God most-high were to create a single substance, it would be free from conjunction, separation, motion, and rest. But as for two substances—if God were to create them—being free from conjunction and separation, and a place and that which occupies a place in the state of remaining being free from motion or rest, that is impossible. And the discussion revolves around this.
Source pages p. 210

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-response unit. The objector (likely a dahri or materialist) argues that if God were to create only one substance, it would be free from motion and rest (since there is no place to move to or from) and free from conjunction and separation (since there is no other substance to conjoin or separate from). The author responds that this objection is actually leveled by the Muslim theologian against those who deny the temporal origination of bodies. The author uses the case of two substances to show that they cannot be free from conjunction and separation, and that a body occupying a place in two moments cannot be free from motion or rest. This is used to prove that all bodies in the world are subject to these accidents, hence they are originated. Once the opponent admits the origination of all bodies, the proof is complete. The author clarifies that they themselves concede that a single substance could be free from these accidents, but that is not the issue—the real discussion is about the actual world which contains multiple substances. The key point is that the impossibility of being free from accidents applies to the actual existents in the world, not hypothetical single substances.

Key Terms
جوهر (substance)
اجتماع (conjunction)
افتراق (separation)
مكان (place)
متمكن (that which occupies a place)
حدوث (temporal origination)
قدم (pre-eternity)
دهري (materialist)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That al-Nasafi denies the possibility of a single substance being free from accidents.
Correction: In fact, he admits it explicitly: 'As for us, we admit that if God most-high were to create a single substance, it would be free from conjunction, separation, motion, and rest.' The argument depends on the actual existence of multiple substances.

Checkpoint
How does al-Nasafi respond to the objection that a single substance could be free from accidents?
A) He rejects the premise that a single substance could be free from accidents.
B) He argues that the objection is irrelevant because the real discussion concerns the actual world with multiple substances.
C) He uses the case of a single substance to prove the opposite.
D) He admits that if a single substance were created, it would still have place, so it would have motion.

Key Takeaway
The objection of a single substance being free from accidents is irrelevant because the proof of temporal origination depends on the actual existence of multiple substances and their necessary relation to accidents.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a truth about your own existence that demands a response, do you create hypothetical 'single substances'—irrelevant objections—to avoid submitting to that truth? What specific objection are you hiding behind today, and will you let it go?
?
This lesson shows that a single hypothetical case does not disprove a universal reality. Where in your relationship with Allah have you used a rare exception to justify ignoring what is clearly required of you? Will you focus on the actual world you live in, or continue arguing about imaginary ones?
?
Al-Nasafi admits a single substance could be free from accidents, but that does not change the conclusion. In what area of your faith do you demand that every possible scenario be explained before you accept the clear evidence? Are you willing to submit to the proofs that are already before you, even if you can think of a clever counterexample?

Al-Nasafi's Dilemma Against Hyle
Al-Nasafi uses a dilemma to refute the doctrine of hyle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Nasafi's dilemma argument that a substance cannot be originally free from both conjunction and separation, and how it refutes the doctrine of hyle.

Core Idea
A substance cannot be free from both conjunction and separation—it leads to a logical dilemma.

Al-Nasafi presents a powerful dilemma to refute the doctrine of hyle (prime matter), which claims that underlying substances are originally devoid of both conjunction and separation. By asking which accident comes first, he shows that both possibilities lead to impossibility. This lesson unpacks this key argument from the Maturidi tradition.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and English carefully to grasp the structure of the argument. Notice the two-part structure: first the general argument, then the application to hyle.
Pay attention to the logical flow of the dilemma.
2
Identify the dilemma
Identify the two options Al-Nasafi gives: either conjunction precedes separation or vice versa, and how each leads to impossibility. The impossibility is because each accident presupposes the other.
Think about why conjunction without prior separation is impossible.
3
Apply the argument to the hyle doctrine
See how Al-Nasafi then applies the same dilemma to the partisans of hyle, showing their position leads to absurdity.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this argument is important in establishing that substances cannot be free from these accidents in pre-eternity. This is a typical kalam argument using rational impossibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نقول لهم : لو كانت هذه الأجسام خالية عن الاجتماع والافتراق ثم حدثا فيها، أي العرضين أسبق إليها الاجتماع أم الافتراق ؟ فأي العرضين عينوا فقد ادعوا محالا، لأن اجتماع ما كان موجودا ولم يكن مفترقا ، أو افتراق ما كان موجودا ولم يكن مجتمعا محال ، وبهذا يبطل قول أصحاب الهيولى أنها من الأزل شيء واحد غير موصوف بالاجتماع والافتراق، فإنا نقول لهم : أي العرضين أسبق إلى الهيولى: الاجتماع أم الافتراق؟ فبأي الجوابين أجابوا كان محالا. والله الموفق.
Then we say to them: If these bodies were free from conjunction and separation and then these two accidents occurred in them, which of the two accidents preceded for them: conjunction or separation? Whichever of the two accidents they specify, they have claimed an impossibility, because the conjunction of something that existed and was not separated, or the separation of something that existed and was not conjoined, is impossible. And by this the statement of the partisans of hyle (ashab al-hayula) is invalidated—that it is from pre-eternity one thing not described by conjunction and separation. So we say to them: Which of the two accidents precedes for the hyle: conjunction or separation? Whichever of the two answers they give, it is impossible. And God is the giver of success.
Source pages p. 211

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of a specific doctrine: that of the proponents of hyle (prime matter), who believe that underlying all physical things is a single substance that is devoid of all qualities, including conjunction and separation, from pre-eternity. Al-Nasafi uses a dilemma argument: if these bodies were originally free from the accidents of conjunction and separation, and then these accidents occur in them, one must ask which accident comes first. If they say conjunction comes first, then the substance would have existed in a state of being separated from nothing, which is impossible because separation presupposes prior conjunction. Similarly, if they say separation comes first, then the substance would have existed in a state of conjunction with nothing, which is also impossible. Thus, the notion that a substance can be free from both these accidents in pre-eternity and then acquire them later is logically untenable. This destroys the doctrine of hyle. The argument is powerful because it forces the opponent to choose between two absurdities.

Key Terms
اجتماع
افتراق
هيولى
عرض
قدم
حدوث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument is about physical motion or temporal sequence.
Correction: It is about logical priority and metaphysical possibility.

Checkpoint
What is the conclusion al-Nasafi draws from the dilemma about which accident precedes in a substance that was originally free from both?
A) That the substance must have had one of them from pre-eternity.
B) That the substance cannot be originally free from both conjunction and separation.
C) That both accidents occur simultaneously.
D) That the substance is not subject to accidents.

Key Takeaway
Al-Nasafi's dilemma shows that a substance cannot be originally free from both conjunction and separation, thus refuting the hyle doctrine.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just saw Al-Nasafi dismantle a false doctrine with a simple logical dilemma. Now think: In your own life, are there beliefs you hold that crumble when you apply this kind of rigorous questioning? Name one assumption you've been holding that you have never truly examined with the same clarity. Will you examine it today, or let it stand unchallenged?
?
The dilemma forces a choice between two impossibilities—exposing a hidden contradiction. What about your own heart: Are there two desires or commitments that logically cannot coexist? You cannot serve both. Which one will you renounce right now?
?
Al-Nasafi’s argument shows that denying both conjunction and separation is unsustainable. In your relationship with Allah, do you try to occupy a middle ground—neither fully obedient nor openly rebellious? That no-man’s land is just as impossible. Which side will you step toward today?

Refutation of the Claim that Eternal Existence is Impossible
Nasafi refutes an opponent's argument about eternal existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical flaw in the claim that eternal existence is impossible for God, and why it leads to unbelief.

Core Idea
Misapplying impossibility leads to denying God's attributes and scripture.

Imam Nasafi here addresses a subtle but dangerous argument that would undermine the eternity of God. An opponent tries to apply a rule about temporal existence to the Creator, leading to the denial of divine power and the Quran. Nasafi exposes the logical error and the disastrous theological consequences.

Guided Path
1
Read the opponent's argument
Read the first part of the passage: 'Just as it is impossible for something that does not precede temporal events to be eternal...' Understand the parallel reasoning.
The opponent assumes a universal rule about impossibility.
2
Identify the fatal conclusion
The opponent concludes that both the world and its Maker are finite, leading him to deny the Quran and accuse God of incapacity.
This is the unintended consequence.
3
Understand the refutation
Nasafi points out the contradiction: if God's power is His essence, then finitude of objects implies finitude of essence, which is unbelief.
4
Reflect on the theological stakes
Consider why this argument is so serious and how it attacks the foundations of tawhid.
The safeguard is recognizing the distinction between Creator and creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في الاستحالة: وقال كما يستحيل قدم ما يستحيل قدام ما لا يسبق الحوادث يستحيل بقاء ما لا ينفك عما يستحيل بقاؤه إلى ما لا يتناهى وجعل كل واحد منهما متناهيا فأداه جهله بالتفرقة بين الأمرين وعجزه عن ذلك إلى إنكار نصوص كتاب الله تعالى وإلى تعجيز الله تعالى مع بقاء ذاته مع أنه يقول: الله تعالى يقدر بقدرة هي ذاته، فإذا كانت قدرته ذاته وقد تناهت القدرة التي هي ذاته لما أن تناهى المقدورات بتناهي القدرة فكان هذا تناهي ذاته لا محالة، وهذا كله كفر صريح عصمنا الله تعالى عن ذلك بفضله.
On impossibility: He said: Just as it is impossible for something that does not precede temporal events to be eternal, it is impossible for something that is not free from that whose eternity is impossible to persist endlessly. And he made each of them finite, so his ignorance of the distinction between the two matters and his inability regarding that led him to deny the texts of the Book of Allah, the Exalted, and to deem Allah incapable while His essence remains, while he says: Allah, the Exalted, has power with a power that is His essence. So if His power is His essence and the power which is His essence has become finite because the objects of power have become finite by the finitude of the power, then this is necessarily the finitude of His essence. And all this is clear unbelief. May Allah protect us from that by His grace.
Source pages p. 217

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an unnamed opponent's argument. The opponent asserts a general rule: just as something that does not precede temporal events cannot be eternal, so something that cannot be free from that which itself cannot be eternal cannot persist endlessly. The opponent then claims both the world and its maker are finite, leading him to deny the revealed texts and to accuse God of incapability, while paradoxically admitting that God's power is His essence. The author points out that if God's power equals His essence, and the objects of power are finite, then the power itself becomes finite, implying the finitude of God's essence. The author declares this conclusion to be outright unbelief and prays for protection.

Key Terms
استحالة
قدم
حوادث
تعجيز
كفر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The impossibility of eternal existence for created things applies equally to the Creator.
Correction: The Creator is fundamentally different; His eternity is necessary, not contingent.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does the opponent's argument ultimately imply about God?
A) That God's essence is eternal
B) That God's essence is finite
C) That God's power is separate from His essence
D) That God cannot create temporal events

Key Takeaway
Misapplying the impossibility of eternal existence to God leads to denying His attributes and scripture, which is clear unbelief.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The opponent's logic led him to deny the clear texts of Allah. Have you ever found yourself twisting or ignoring a verse of the Qur'an because it clashed with your own reasoning? What would it take for you to submit fully to revelation, even when your mind cannot fully grasp it?
?
If someone argued that God's power is limited because the objects of His power are finite, how would you respond using the insights from this lesson? Is your trust in God's absolute power truly reflected in how you face your daily limitations?
?
The opponent's error stemmed from misapplying a rule. In what area of your life might you be applying human standards to Allah's attributes, and how can you train your heart to affirm His perfection without reduction?

Why God's Eternal Attributes Do Not Make the World Eternal
Nasafi refutes the argument that the world is eternal because God's attributes are eternal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the eternity of God's attributes does not logically entail the eternity of the world.

Core Idea
Eternal cause does not necessitate eternal effect.

Imam Nasafi refutes materialists who argue that because God's attributes (existence, generosity, knowledge, power, wisdom) are eternal, the world must also be eternal. He demonstrates that this reasoning is flawed because temporal accidents can be known through sensation without implying the eternity of their causes. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation, noting the materialists' claim and Nasafi's response.
Pay attention to the structure: claim, refutation, and alternative explanation.
2
Identify the materialist claim
The materialists say the world is eternal because its cause (God's attributes) is eternal. What is the specific reasoning?
3
Understand Nasafi's refutation
Nasafi says we have proven temporality of some accidents by sensation, and that does not require that the essence or attributes of God be temporal. So the attribute's eternity does not transfer to the world.
He uses an analogy: the temporality of accidents does not affect the eternity of God.
4
Reflect on the logical flaw
The materialists commit a non sequitur: they assume that if the cause is eternal, the effect must be eternal. Nasafi shows this is not necessary.
This lesson guards against that misconception.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
جئنا إلى حل شبهات الدهرية، فأما القائلون منهم بانه قديم لأن الباري تعالى علة وجوده أو جوده أو علمه أو قدرته أو حكمته على نحو ما مر من بيان أقاويلهم فهو في غاية الفساد، لأنا بينا دلالة الحدوث، وثبتنا ذلك بما يوجب العلم به قطعا يقينا وفي ثبوت ذلك ظهور بطلان القول بقدمه ألا ترى أنا ثبتنا حدوث بعض الأعراض في الحال على وجه يعرف ذلك بالحس، ولم يقتض قدم ذات صانعه ولا قدم جوده أو علمه أو قدرته أو حكمه قدم تلك الأعراض. وظهر بهذا أن قدم ما به تعلق وجود العالم لم يوجب قدمه نحقق ذلك : أن هؤلاء كلهم أقروا بكون العالم مصنوعا ثم ادعوا قدمه لكون ما يتعلق وجوده به قديما، والقديم يمتنع تعلق ذاته بآخر يوجده، وكيف يتصور ذلك وقط لم يكن ذاته غير موجود، والقول بقدم ما تعلق وجوده بغير مناقضة ظاهرة إذ القديم ما يستغني في وجوده عن غيره والمحدث ما يتعلق وجوده بغيره فهم إذا قالوا: هو قديم وقالوا مع ذلك أن وجوده تعلق بغيره، صاروا قائلين أنه قديم محدث وهو متناقض، لأنه يصير كأنهم قالوا: العالم لا ابتداء لوجوده ومع هذا لوجوده ابتداء وعرف بما سبق ذكره بطلان قول القائلين: إن العالم قديم الطينة حديث الصنعة، فإنا بينا استحالة القول بأصل للعالم متعر عن الحوادث.
We come to solving the doubts of the materialists. As for those among them who say that it (the world) is eternal because the Creator, Exalted, is the cause of its existence or His generosity or His knowledge or His power or His wisdom, according to what has passed of the exposition of their statements, it is extremely corrupt. Because we have shown the proof of temporality, and we have established that by what obligates certain knowledge of it, and in the establishment of that is the manifestation of the invalidity of the statement of its eternity. Do you not see that we have established the temporality of some accidents at the present moment in a way known by sensation, and the eternity of the essence of its Maker did not entail, nor did the eternity of His generosity or His knowledge or His power or His wisdom entail the eternity of those accidents. And it became clear by this that the eternity of that by which the existence of the world is related did not necessitate its eternity. We realize that: all these people acknowledged that the world is made, then they claimed its eternity because that to which its existence is related is eternal. And the eternal is impossible for its essence to be related to another that brings it into existence. And how can that be imagined when its essence has never been non-existent? And the statement that something eternal has its existence related to another is a clear contradiction, because the eternal is what is independent in its existence from other than it, and the temporal is what has its existence related to other than it. So if they say: it is eternal, and they say along with that that its existence is related to another, they become saying that it is eternal and temporal, which is contradictory, because it becomes as if they said: the world has no beginning for its existence, and along with this it has a beginning for its existence. And it is known by what has preceded of mention the invalidity of the statement of those who say: the world is eternal in matter, temporal in make, for we have shown the impossibility of the statement that there is a substratum for the world devoid of temporal events.
Source pages p. 218

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of two materialist (دهرية) positions. First, those who argue the world is eternal because God's attributes (existence, generosity, knowledge, power, wisdom) are eternal. The author counters that proving the temporality of some sensible accidents does not entail the eternity of the world, and the eternity of the attributes does not necessitate the eternity of the world. Second, the author notes that all materialists admit the world is made, yet claim its eternity on the grounds that its cause is eternal. This creates a contradiction: the eternal cannot be dependent on another for its existence, while the temporal is dependent. Thus, claiming the world is both eternal and dependent is contradictory, akin to saying it has no beginning and a beginning. The author also references a previous refutation of those who say the world is eternal in matter but temporal in form, deeming it impossible to have a substratum devoid of temporal events.

Key Terms
دهرية
قدم
حدوث
باري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The world must be eternal because its creator (God) is eternal.
Correction: Eternity of the cause does not logically imply eternity of the effect. Temporal accidents demonstrate that an eternal cause can produce temporal effects.

Checkpoint
What is the main logical flaw that Nasafi identifies in the materialists' argument?
A) They assume the world is created.
B) They infer that because the cause is eternal, the effect must be eternal.
C) They deny the existence of accidents.
D) They confuse absolute attributes with relational ones.

Key Takeaway
God's eternal attributes do not force the world to be eternal; temporal effects can proceed from an eternal cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that because God is all-powerful, your difficulties must be eternal? What would change if you truly believed that His eternal power can produce temporary trials?
?
Nasafi refutes the materialists using sensory evidence of change. When was the last time you let the obvious signs of creation—like the passing of time—remind you that the world is not eternal and that you are accountable?
?
The materialist fallacy was rooted in a false sense of cause and effect. Examine your own faith: are there any beliefs you hold about God that are actually based on a logical leap? Will you commit to testing your assumptions against revelation?

The Contradiction of a Created Eternal World
Nasafi exposes the contradiction in claiming the world is both created and eternal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical contradiction in claiming the world is both created (temporal) and eternal.

Core Idea
Created and eternal cannot coexist; it is a contradiction.

Imam Nasafi continues his refutation by exposing an internal contradiction in the materialist position. They admit the world is made (created) yet claim it is eternal. Since the eternal cannot depend on another for existence, and the temporal does depend, saying the world is both is contradictory. This lesson analyzes that contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the second part of the source, focusing on the logic of dependency.
2
Identify the materialists' admission
Nasafi notes that materialists acknowledge the world is created (مصنوع). But then they claim it is eternal because its cause is eternal.
This is the key point of tension.
3
Understand the definitions of eternal and temporal
Eternal: independent in existence. Temporal: dependent on another. Nasafi uses these definitions to show the contradiction.
4
Recognize the contradiction
If the world is eternal, it cannot be dependent; but if it is created, it is dependent. Thus claiming both is contradictory.
Nasafi summarizes: it is like saying the world has no beginning and also has a beginning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

?

?
Nasafi calls it a clear contradiction to claim the world is both eternal and created. Are there areas in your faith or daily practice where you hold contradictory beliefs—for example, trusting in Allah's provision while anxiety drives you to hoard? What would it take for you to root out such contradictions?

Nasafi's Refutation of Mu'tazila's Reliance on Estimation
Nasafi criticizes Mu'tazila for relying on estimation over reason.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Nasafi's critique that the Mu'tazila erroneously relied on estimation (wahm) instead of reason, causing them to reject the rational possibility of creation from nothing.

Core Idea
Estimation is not a reliable basis for theology; reason must govern.

In this passage, Nasafi targets a fundamental methodological error of the Mu'tazila: they based many of their doctrines on estimation (wahm) rather than on rational concepts. This led them to deny that something can come from nothing—aligning them with the materialist Dahriyya. The text is fragmented but conveys a sharp critique that underscores the importance of distinguishing between the imagined and the rational.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic text and its English translation to get a sense of Nasafi's concise argument.
The Arabic is incomplete and may contain a textual corruption; treat it as a fragment.
2
Identify the key accusation
Notice that Nasafi accuses the Mu'tazila of relying on estimation (wahm) in most of their doctrines and of excluding what is not imagined from being rationally conceivable.
This distinction between the imagined (mawhum) and the rational (ma'qul) was established in an earlier lesson.
3
Understand the consequence
Because of this confusion, the Mu'tazila ended up affirming the impossibility of something from nothing, thus agreeing with the Dahriyya (materialists) who denied creation ex nihilo.
This shows how a methodological mistake leads to a serious theological error.
4
Reflect on the broader lesson
Consider why distinguishing between wahm and reason is crucial in kalam. How does relying on estimation distort theological reasoning?
Nasafi's critique reminds us that rational concepts must be based on sound intellectual grounds, not mere mental imagery.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والرد على المعتزلة والمعتزلة لاعتمادهم في أكثر مذاهبهم على الوهم وإخراجهم ما ليس بموهوم عن كونه معقولا جهلا منهم بما بينا من التفرقة بين الأمرين ما لو إلى القول باستحالة وجود الشيء لا من شيء وصوبوا الدهرية في ذلك، وادعوا القول بقدم
And the refutation of the Mu'tazila. And the Mu'tazila, due to their reliance in most of their doctrines on estimation (al-wahm), and their exclusion of what is not imagined by estimation from being rationally conceivable, out of ignorance of the distinction between the two matters which we have explained, they have come to affirm the impossibility of the existence of something from nothing, and they approved the Dahriyya in that, and they claimed the doctrine of the eternity
Source pages p. 220

Commentary
This passage begins a refutation of the Mu'tazila. Nasafi accuses them of relying on estimation (wahm) rather than reason in most of their doctrines. As a result, they exclude what is not imagined by estimation from being rationally conceivable, which shows their ignorance of the distinction between the sensed and the rational (explained in the previous lesson). This leads them to agree with the Dahriyya (materialists) that something cannot come from nothing. The text is cut off but indicates that the Mu'tazila claimed the eternity (presumably of the world or something else). The refutation continues beyond the page.

Key Terms

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila always base their theology on pure reason and rational arguments.
Correction: Nasafi argues that in fact they rely on estimation (wahm) rather than reason, leading them to reject rational concepts like creation ex nihilo.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, what error does the Mu'tazila share with the Dahriyya?
A) They deny the existence of God.
B) They accept the possibility of something from nothing.
C) They reject the possibility of something from nothing.
D) They follow the Qur'an literally.

Key Takeaway
Nasafi warns that when theologians substitute estimation for reason, they risk denying fundamental doctrines such as creation ex nihilo.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever rejected a truth of the faith simply because your imagination could not picture it? What does that reveal about the faculty you trusted over revelation?
?
When you face a doctrine that seems impossible to your mind, do you immediately dismiss it or pause to ask whether your 'reason' might actually be estimation in disguise? How can you train yourself to distinguish the two?
?
If the Mu'tazila—known for their intellectual rigor—could be led astray by estimation, how much more vulnerable are you? What practical step will you take this week to submit your thoughts to the authority of revelation rather than your own imagination?

The Mu'tazila's Error: Reliance on Imagination and the Claim of the Nonexistent as a Thing
The author exposes the Mu'tazila's error and their claim about the nonexistent as a thing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Mu'tazila's reliance on imagination led them to claim that the world in non-existence was a 'thing' (shay'), and why the author refutes this view.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila confused imagination with reason, leading them to a false doctrine about creation.

The Mu'tazila, known for their rationalism, fell into a subtle error by relying on imagination (wahm) instead of pure reason. They claimed that the world, even in its state of non-existence, was still a 'thing' (shay'). This lesson examines their reasoning, their motive, and the author's critique.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Mu'tazila's error
Read the passage and note how the author describes their reliance on imagination and their exclusion of the non-imagined from the rational.
The author distinguishes between imagination and reason.
2
Understand their claim about the nonexistent
Focus on their statement: 'the world was non-existent except that it was a thing'. What does this mean?
They asserted that 'thing' applies to the nonexistent.
3
Consider their motive
Why did they make this claim? The passage mentions fear of the sword or desire to reassure the ignorant.
This shows their doctrine was not purely intellectual but had external pressures.
4
See the author's refutation
The author refutes by showing that their view leads to impossibility (creation from nothing) and aligns with the Dahriyya.
The author has previously distinguished between imagination and reason.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرد على المعتزلة والمعتزلة لاعتمادهم في أكثر مذاهبهم على الوهم وإخراجهم ما ليس بموهوم عن كونه معقولا جهلا منهم بما بينا من التفرقة بين الأمرين ما لو [?] إلى القول باستحالة وجود الشيء لا من شيء وصوبوا الدهرية في ذلك، وادعوا القول بقدم الأشياء. وخافوا من معرة السيف، أو قصدوا تقرير من لا خبرة له بمعرفة الحقائق فموهوا وقالوا: أنا نقول بأن العالم كان معدوما إلا أنه كان شيئا وإن الباري جل وعلا أوجده من العدم لا أن جعل ما ليس بشيء شيئا إذ هو محال. ثم اقتصر على هذا القدر، وزاد الجبائي وجماعة من البصريين فزعموا أن ما كان في حالة الوجود عرضا يكون في حالة العدم أيضا عرضا، وكذا ما كان لونا أو طعما أو حركة أو سكونا أو جوهرا فهو في حالة العدم كذلك. فكانت الجواهر والأعراض والألوان والأكوان والطعوم كذلك في الأزل، وزاد أبو الحسن الخياط أحد رؤساء البغداديين من المعتزلة وهو أستاذ الكعبي، فزعم أن ما كان في حالة الوجود جسما يكون في حالة العدم أيضا، وأصل لنفسه أصلا فزعم أن ما لا يستحيل أن يكون الموجود موصوفا به في أول أحوال وجوده، لا يستحيل اتصافه به في حالة العدم، والعرض في أول أحوال وجوده يوصف بأنه عرض، وكذا كل نوع فيه كالحركة توصف بكونها حركة في تلك الحال، وكذا السكون واللون والطعم
Refutation of the Mu'tazila. The Mu'tazila, due to their reliance in most of their doctrines on imagination and their exclusion of what is not imagined from being rational—out of ignorance of the distinction we have clarified between the two matters—led [?] them to the statement of the impossibility of the existence of a thing from nothing, and they endorsed the Dahriyya in that, and claimed the pre-eternality of things. They feared the disgrace of the sword, or intended to reassure those with no expertise in knowing realities, so they obfuscated and said: 'We say that the world was non-existent, except that it was a thing, and that the Creator, exalted, brought it into existence from non-existence, not that He made what is not a thing into a thing, for that is impossible.' Then al-Ka'bi limited himself to this extent. Al-Jubba'i and a group of the Basrans added, claiming that what in the state of existence is an accident is also an accident in the state of non-existence, and likewise what is a color, taste, motion, rest, or substance is likewise in the state of non-existence. So substances, accidents, colors, modes, and tastes were thus in pre-eternity. And Abu al-Hasan al-Khayyat, one of the chief Baghdadis among the Mu'tazila and the teacher of al-Ka'bi, added, claiming that what in the state of existence is a body is also a body in the state of non-existence. He established for himself a principle: he claimed that whatever it is not impossible for an existent to be described with in the first state of its existence, it is not impossible for it to be described with in the state of non-existence. An accident in the first state of its existence is described as an accident, and likewise every type in it, like motion described as being motion in that state, and likewise rest, color, and taste.
Source pages p. 220

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazila's doctrines regarding the nonexistent (ma'dum). The author traces their error back to their reliance on imagination (wahm) and their failure to distinguish the rational from the imagined, which led them to deny creation ex nihilo and to agree with the Dahriyya (materialists) that the world is pre-eternal. He then explains their motive: either fear of persecution (the sword) or a desire to mislead the ignorant. To justify their position, they claimed that the world was non-existent but was still a 'thing' (shay'), and that God brought it into existence from non-existence, not by making a non-thing into a thing (since they deem that impossible). The author then presents the range of Mu'tazila views on what the nonexistent thing is. Al-Ka'bi stopped at the general claim. Al-Jubba'i and other Basrans added that an accident in existence is also an accident in non-existence, and similarly for colors, tastes, motions, rests, and substances—so these categories existed as such in pre-eternity. Abu al-Hasan al-Khayyat went further, claiming that a body in existence is also a body in non-existence. He established a principle: if a description is possible for an existent in its first state of existence, then it is possible for it in the state of non-existence. He argued that an accident is described as an accident in its first state, so it must be an accident in non-existence; the same for motion, rest, color, and taste. The author implicitly rejects these views as absurd, since they entail the pre-eternality of contingent entities.

Key Terms
المعدوم (ma'dum)
الشيء (shay')
الوهم (wahm)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila believed that the nonexistent world was a 'thing' (shay') and that this was a rational position.
Correction: The author shows that this belief stems from confusing imagination with reason and leads to the heresy of pre-eternity.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what led the Mu'tazila to their claim about the nonexistent?
A) Their reliance on imagination
B) Their study of physics
C) Their commitment to divine attributes
D) Their interpretation of Quran

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila's claim that the nonexistent world is a 'thing' is based on a flawed reliance on imagination, not reason.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hold a belief about God or creation, how do you know it comes from revelation and reason rather than from your own imagination or wishful thinking? Examine one such belief today and ask: 'Is this from the Book and Sunnah, or from my own mind?'
?
The Mu'tazila feared 'the disgrace of the sword' and tailored their doctrine to avoid persecution or to reassure others. Is there a truth you have softened or withheld because you were afraid of how people would react? Confess that to yourself and commit to speaking the truth with wisdom but without compromise.
?
The author says the Mu'tazila confused imagination with reason. Where in your life do you mistake your emotions or assumptions for sound rational judgment? What concrete step can you take this week to train yourself to distinguish between the two?

The Mu'tazila's Diversity: Al-Jubba'i and Al-Khayyat on the Nonexistent
The author details the specific Mu'tazila positions on the nature of the nonexistent thing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the specific positions of al-Jubba'i and al-Khayyat regarding the properties of the nonexistent (ma'dum) and their implications.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila extended their error by assigning specific categories—accident, substance, body—to the nonexistent.

Having established that the nonexistent is a 'thing', some Mu'tazila went further to specify its nature. Al-Jubba'i and the Basrans claimed that an accident in existence remains an accident in non-existence, while al-Khayyat extended this to bodies. This lesson explores these views and the principles behind them.

Guided Path
1
Review al-Jubba'i's addition
Read the part about al-Jubba'i and the Basrans. They claim that what is an accident in existence is also an accident in non-existence, and similarly for colors, tastes, etc.
This implies that accidents and substances existed as such in pre-eternity.
2
Understand al-Khayyat's extension
Al-Khayyat goes further to claim that a body in existence is also a body in non-existence. Note his principle: if a description is possible in the first state of existence, it is possible in non-existence.
He uses the example of an accident being described as an accident in its first state.
3
See the absurd consequences
The author presents these views to show how they lead to the pre-eternality of contingent entities, which is against Islamic theology.
This reinforces the refutation of the Mu'tazila.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرد على المعتزلة والمعتزلة لاعتمادهم في أكثر مذاهبهم على الوهم وإخراجهم ما ليس بموهوم عن كونه معقولا جهلا منهم بما بينا من التفرقة بين الأمرين ما لو [?] إلى القول باستحالة وجود الشيء لا من شيء وصوبوا الدهرية في ذلك، وادعوا القول بقدم الأشياء. وخافوا من معرة السيف، أو قصدوا تقرير من لا خبرة له بمعرفة الحقائق فموهوا وقالوا: أنا نقول بأن العالم كان معدوما إلا أنه كان شيئا وإن الباري جل وعلا أوجده من العدم لا أن جعل ما ليس بشيء شيئا إذ هو محال. ثم اقتصر على هذا القدر، وزاد الجبائي وجماعة من البصريين فزعموا أن ما كان في حالة الوجود عرضا يكون في حالة العدم أيضا عرضا، وكذا ما كان لونا أو طعما أو حركة أو سكونا أو جوهرا فهو في حالة العدم كذلك. فكانت الجواهر والأعراض والألوان والأكوان والطعوم كذلك في الأزل، وزاد أبو الحسن الخياط أحد رؤساء البغداديين من المعتزلة وهو أستاذ الكعبي، فزعم أن ما كان في حالة الوجود جسما يكون في حالة العدم أيضا، وأصل لنفسه أصلا فزعم أن ما لا يستحيل أن يكون الموجود موصوفا به في أول أحوال وجوده، لا يستحيل اتصافه به في حالة العدم، والعرض في أول أحوال وجوده يوصف بأنه عرض، وكذا كل نوع فيه كالحركة توصف بكونها حركة في تلك الحال، وكذا السكون واللون والطعم
Refutation of the Mu'tazila. The Mu'tazila, due to their reliance in most of their doctrines on imagination and their exclusion of what is not imagined from being rational—out of ignorance of the distinction we have clarified between the two matters—led [?] them to the statement of the impossibility of the existence of a thing from nothing, and they endorsed the Dahriyya in that, and claimed the pre-eternality of things. They feared the disgrace of the sword, or intended to reassure those with no expertise in knowing realities, so they obfuscated and said: 'We say that the world was non-existent, except that it was a thing, and that the Creator, exalted, brought it into existence from non-existence, not that He made what is not a thing into a thing, for that is impossible.' Then al-Ka'bi limited himself to this extent. Al-Jubba'i and a group of the Basrans added, claiming that what in the state of existence is an accident is also an accident in the state of non-existence, and likewise what is a color, taste, motion, rest, or substance is likewise in the state of non-existence. So substances, accidents, colors, modes, and tastes were thus in pre-eternity. And Abu al-Hasan al-Khayyat, one of the chief Baghdadis among the Mu'tazila and the teacher of al-Ka'bi, added, claiming that what in the state of existence is a body is also a body in the state of non-existence. He established for himself a principle: he claimed that whatever it is not impossible for an existent to be described with in the first state of its existence, it is not impossible for it to be described with in the state of non-existence. An accident in the first state of its existence is described as an accident, and likewise every type in it, like motion described as being motion in that state, and likewise rest, color, and taste.
Source pages p. 220

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazila's doctrines regarding the nonexistent (ma'dum). The author traces their error back to their reliance on imagination (wahm) and their failure to distinguish the rational from the imagined, which led them to deny creation ex nihilo and to agree with the Dahriyya (materialists) that the world is pre-eternal. He then explains their motive: either fear of persecution (the sword) or a desire to mislead the ignorant. To justify their position, they claimed that the world was non-existent but was still a 'thing' (shay'), and that God brought it into existence from non-existence, not by making a non-thing into a thing (since they deem that impossible). The author then presents the range of Mu'tazila views on what the nonexistent thing is. Al-Ka'bi stopped at the general claim. Al-Jubba'i and other Basrans added that an accident in existence is also an accident in non-existence, and similarly for colors, tastes, motions, rests, and substances—so these categories existed as such in pre-eternity. Abu al-Hasan al-Khayyat went further, claiming that a body in existence is also a body in non-existence. He established a principle: if a description is possible for an existent in its first state of existence, then it is possible for it in the state of non-existence. He argued that an accident is described as an accident in its first state, so it must be an accident in non-existence; the same for motion, rest, color, and taste. The author implicitly rejects these views as absurd, since they entail the pre-eternality of contingent entities.

Key Terms
الجوهر (jawhar)
العرض ('arad)
الجسم (jism)
الأزل (pre-eternity)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila believed that the categories of existence (accident, substance, body) apply to the nonexistent as they do to the existent.
Correction: The author shows that this leads to the absurdity of pre-eternal accidents and bodies.

Checkpoint
What did Abu al-Hasan al-Khayyat claim about bodies in non-existence?
A) They are not bodies
B) They are bodies
C) They are accidents
D) They are nothing

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila's view that the nonexistent retains its categorical properties leads to the claim of pre-eternal accidents and bodies, which the author refutes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you plan your future, do you sometimes treat those plans as already fixed realities, forgetting that they only exist by Allah's will? How does the Mu'tazila's error of assigning categories to the nonexistent mirror a subtle form of assuming control over what is not yet created by Allah? What is one area where you need to renew your tawakkul today?
?
The Mu'tazila's logical principle seemed sound to them, yet it led to a grave error. Where in your own understanding of faith do you rely on a seemingly logical but actually flawed assumption? Take a moment to ask Allah to guide your intellect to the truth and protect you from hidden errors in your reasoning.
?
Al-Khayyat claimed that what is true of a thing at first existence must be true in non-existence. But non-existence is total absence. In your life, do you sometimes hold onto what has passed as if it still has a claim on you, when in reality it no longer exists except in Allah's knowledge? What past regret or grievance can you truly accept as non-existent in terms of its power over you, and move forward in submission to Allah?

The Mu'tazilite Claim: The Non-Existent is a Thing
Al-Nasafi reports the initial Mu'tazilite claim and its motivation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the basic Mu'tazilite claim that the non-existent (al-ma'dum) is a 'thing' (shay') and the motivation behind this claim.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazilites argued that God created from a non-existent that was a 'thing', not from absolute nothing.

This passage opens Imam al-Nasafi's discussion of Mu'tazilite views on the non-existent. The Mu'tazilites were concerned to avoid the implication that God created from absolute nothingness, which they considered impossible. They therefore claimed that the world was non-existent yet was a 'thing' in pre-eternity. This lesson explains their initial position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the Mu'tazilite claim.
Notice the phrase 'إلا أنه كان شيئا'.
2
Identify the motivation
The passage mentions two motivations: fear of the sword's disgrace, or desire to convince the ignorant. Consider why they would say this.
3
Understand the distinction
They distinguish between creating from non-existence and making what is not a thing into a thing. Understand why they think the latter is impossible.
This distinction is key to their theology.
4
Reflect on the limitation
The text says 'ثم اقتصر على هذا القدر' meaning al-Ka'bi limited himself to this claim. Reflect on why he did not add more.
Later Mu'tazilites added more attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وخافوا من معرة السيف، أو قصدوا تقرير من لا خبرة له بمعرفة الحقائق فموهوا وقالوا: أنا نقول بأن العالم كان معدوما إلا أنه كان شيئا وإن الباري جل وعلا أوجده من العدم لا أن جعل ما ليس بشيء شيئا إذ هو محال. ثم اقتصر على هذا القدر، وزاد الجبائي وجماعة من البصريين فزعموا أن ما كان في حالة الوجود عرضا يكون في حالة العدم أيضا عرضا، وكذا ما كان لونا أو طعما أو حركة أو سكونا أو جوهرا فهو في حالة العدم كذلك. فكانت الجواهر والأعراض والألوان والأكوان والطعوم كذلك في الأزل، وزاد أبو الحسن الخياط أحد رؤساء البغداديين من المعتزلة وهو أستاذ الكعبي، فزعم أن ما كان في حالة الوجود جسما يكون في حالة العدم أيضا، وأصل لنفسه أصلا فزعم أن ما لا يستحيل أن يكون الموجود موصوفا به في أول أحوال وجوده، لا يستحيل اتصافه به في حالة العدم، والعرض في أول أحوال وجوده يوصف بأنه عرض، وكذا كل نوع فيه كالحركة توصف بكونها حركة في تلك الحال، وكذا السكون واللون والطعم والجوهر والجسم. فأما الموجود فيستحيل اتصافه في أول أحوال وجوده بأنه متحرك أو ساكن أو ماش أو قاعد أو أكل أو شارب، فيستحيل أيضا اتصافه بها في حالة العدم.
And they feared the disgrace of the sword, or intended to reinforce one who has no expertise in knowing realities, so they confused and said: 'We say that the world was non-existent except that it was a thing, and that the Exalted and Glorious Creator brought it into existence from non-existence, not that He made what is not a thing into a thing, for that is impossible.' Then he limited himself to that extent, and al-Jubba'i and a group of the Basrans added, claiming that what in the state of existence is an accident is also an accident in the state of non-existence, and likewise what is a color or a taste or a motion or a rest or a substance, it is so in the state of non-existence. Thus substances, accidents, colors, modes, and tastes were so in pre-eternity. And Abu al-Hasan al-Khayyat, one of the leaders of the Baghdadis among the Mu'tazila and the teacher of al-Ka'bi, added, claiming that what in the state of existence is a body is also a body in the state of non-existence, and he laid down a principle for himself, claiming that what is not impossible for the existent to be described with in the first state of its existence is not impossible for it to be described with in the state of non-existence. The accident in the first state of its existence is described as an accident, and likewise every type within it, like motion, is described as being motion in that state, and likewise rest, color, taste, substance, and body. As for the existent, it is impossible for it to be described in the first state of its existence as moving or resting or walking or sitting or eating or drinking; it is also impossible for it to be described with them in the state of non-existence.
Source pages p. 221

Commentary
This passage reports the theological positions of various Mu'tazilite scholars regarding the nature of the non-existent (al-ma'dum). It begins by explaining the motivation of some Mu'tazilites who, out of fear or in order to convince the ignorant, claimed that the world was non-existent yet was a 'thing' (shay'), and that God brought it from non-existence, not that He made what was not a thing into a thing. Then it reports that al-Ka'bi limited himself to this claim. Al-Jubba'i and the Basran Mu'tazilites added that what in existence is an accident remains an accident in non-existence, and similarly for colors, tastes, motions, rests, and substances; thus these categories pre-existed eternally as non-existent entities. Al-Khayyat went further, claiming that what in existence is a body remains a body in non-existence, and he established a principle: whatever attribute is not impossible for an existent to have in the first instant of its existence is not impossible for it to have in non-existence. Thus accidents are described as accidents in their first state, and so on for types. However, the passage then distinguishes the existent: it cannot be described with contingent actions (moving, sitting, etc.) in its first state, so it also cannot be described with them in non-existence. The author here is presenting these views for later refutation.

الشيء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What did the Mu'tazilites claim about the world before creation?
A) It was absolutely nothing.
B) It was non-existent but a thing.
C) It was a body.
D) It was an accident.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilites held that the non-existent world was a 'thing' in pre-eternity, to avoid the impossibility of creating from absolute nothingness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a theological puzzle, do you tend to adjust your understanding to fit your own reasoning, or do you submit to revelation even if it doesn't make full sense to you? The Mu'tazilites chose the former—where do you stand right now?
?
The Mu'tazilites were motivated either by fear (of the sword) or by a desire to make the faith easier for the ignorant. Have you ever watered down a core Islamic belief to avoid an uncomfortable conversation or to make yourself more accepted? Think of one specific situation and what you would do differently.
?
If God truly creates from absolute nothing, then nothing is impossible for Him. But do you live as if you believe that, or do you subconsciously put limits on His power in your daily life—your fears, your plans, your hopes? What would change if you truly internalized this lesson?

Al-Jubba'i and the Basrans: Attributes in Non-Existence
Al-Nasafi reports al-Jubba'i and the Basrans' position on the non-existent retaining its specific quiddity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Jubba'i and the Basran Mu'tazilites' extension of the non-existent's attributes to accidents, substances, colors, tastes, motions, and rests.

Core Idea
Al-Jubba'i taught that whatever an entity is in existence, it remains the same in non-existence.

Building on the basic claim that the non-existent is a thing, al-Jubba'i and the Basran Mu'tazilites specified that the non-existent retains its specific ontological category. If in existence it is an accident, in non-existence it is still an accident; if a substance, then a substance. This lesson explores this extension and its implications for pre-eternal entities.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation for al-Jubba'i's addition.
Note the list: color, taste, motion, rest, substance.
2
Identify the categories
The passage mentions that what is an accident in existence remains an accident in non-existence. Similarly for color, taste, motion, rest, and substance.
This implies these categories are eternal in their quiddity.
3
Understand the implication
The text concludes: 'فكانت الجواهر والأعراض والألوان والأكوان والطعوم كذلك في الأزل' — thus substances, accidents, colors, modes, and tastes were so in pre-eternity.
'أكوان' refers to modes of being like motion and rest.
4
Reflect on the difference from the basic claim
Al-Jubba'i added specific attributes, whereas the earlier claim only said the world was a thing. Discuss why he felt the need to specify.
This shows the internal diversity among Mu'tazilites.

Al-Khayyat: Bodies and the Principle of Attribute Permanence
Al-Nasafi reports al-Khayyat's claim about bodies and his principle of attribute permanence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Khayyat's extension to bodies and his principle of attribute permanence, as well as the limitation regarding contingent actions.

Core Idea
Al-Khayyat claimed that even bodies remain bodies in non-existence, based on a principle that any attribute possible for an existent at its first moment is also possible in non-existence.

The most extensive Mu'tazilite claim is attributed to Abu al-Hasan al-Khayyat, who argued that bodies, like substances and accidents, remain bodies in non-existence. He also formulated a general principle: whatever can be attributed to an existent in the first state of its existence can also be attributed to it in non-existence. However, this principle has a limitation: contingent actions like moving and sitting cannot be attributed to the existent in its first state, so they are not attributed in non-existence either.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read al-Khayyat's addition and his principle.
He was a leader of the Baghdad Mu'tazila.
2
Identify the principle
Al-Khayyat states that if an attribute is not impossible for an existent in the first state of its existence, it is not impossible in non-existence.
This is a key methodological principle.
3
See how the principle applies
He applies it to accidents, motion, rest, color, taste, substance, and body. All these are attributes that can be ascribed to an existent at its first moment.
4
Note the exception
The passage then distinguishes contingent actions (moving, sitting, eating, etc.) that cannot be ascribed to an existent in its first state. Therefore they also cannot be ascribed in non-existence.
This shows the limit of the principle.
5
Synthesize the Mu'tazilite views
Compare this to the earlier views. Al-Khayyat extended to bodies; what does that imply about the nature of the non-existent?
Think about the ontological status of bodies before creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وخافوا من معرة السيف، أو قصدوا تقرير من لا خبرة له بمعرفة الحقائق فموهوا وقالوا: أنا نقول بأن العالم كان معدوما إلا أنه كان شيئا وإن الباري جل وعلا أوجده من العدم لا أن جعل ما ليس بشيء شيئا إذ هو محال. ثم اقتصر على هذا القدر، وزاد الجبائي وجماعة من البصريين فزعموا أن ما كان في حالة الوجود عرضا يكون في حالة العدم أيضا عرضا، وكذا ما كان لونا أو طعما أو حركة أو سكونا أو جوهرا فهو في حالة العدم كذلك. فكانت الجواهر والأعراض والألوان والأكوان والطعوم كذلك في الأزل، وزاد أبو الحسن الخياط أحد رؤساء البغداديين من المعتزلة وهو أستاذ الكعبي، فزعم أن ما كان في حالة الوجود جسما يكون في حالة العدم أيضا، وأصل لنفسه أصلا فزعم أن ما لا يستحيل أن يكون الموجود موصوفا به في أول أحوال وجوده، لا يستحيل اتصافه به في حالة العدم، والعرض في أول أحوال وجوده يوصف بأنه عرض، وكذا كل نوع فيه كالحركة توصف بكونها حركة في تلك الحال، وكذا السكون واللون والطعم والجوهر والجسم. فأما الموجود فيستحيل اتصافه في أول أحوال وجوده بأنه متحرك أو ساكن أو ماش أو قاعد أو أكل أو شارب، فيستحيل أيضا اتصافه بها في حالة العدم.
And they feared the disgrace of the sword, or intended to reinforce one who has no expertise in knowing realities, so they confused and said: 'We say that the world was non-existent except that it was a thing, and that the Exalted and Glorious Creator brought it into existence from non-existence, not that He made what is not a thing into a thing, for that is impossible.' Then he limited himself to that extent, and al-Jubba'i and a group of the Basrans added, claiming that what in the state of existence is an accident is also an accident in the state of non-existence, and likewise what is a color or a taste or a motion or a rest or a substance, it is so in the state of non-existence. Thus substances, accidents, colors, modes, and tastes were so in pre-eternity. And Abu al-Hasan al-Khayyat, one of the leaders of the Baghdadis among the Mu'tazila and the teacher of al-Ka'bi, added, claiming that what in the state of existence is a body is also a body in the state of non-existence, and he laid down a principle for himself, claiming that what is not impossible for the existent to be described with in the first state of its existence is not impossible for it to be described with in the state of non-existence. The accident in the first state of its existence is described as an accident, and likewise every type within it, like motion, is described as being motion in that state, and likewise rest, color, taste, substance, and body. As for the existent, it is impossible for it to be described in the first state of its existence as moving or resting or walking or sitting or eating or drinking; it is also impossible for it to be described with them in the state of non-existence.
Source pages p. 221

Commentary
This passage reports the theological positions of various Mu'tazilite scholars regarding the nature of the non-existent (al-ma'dum). It begins by explaining the motivation of some Mu'tazilites who, out of fear or in order to convince the ignorant, claimed that the world was non-existent yet was a 'thing' (shay'), and that God brought it from non-existence, not that He made what was not a thing into a thing. Then it reports that al-Ka'bi limited himself to this claim. Al-Jubba'i and the Basran Mu'tazilites added that what in existence is an accident remains an accident in non-existence, and similarly for colors, tastes, motions, rests, and substances; thus these categories pre-existed eternally as non-existent entities. Al-Khayyat went further, claiming that what in existence is a body remains a body in non-existence, and he established a principle: whatever attribute is not impossible for an existent to have in the first instant of its existence is not impossible for it to have in non-existence. Thus accidents are described as accidents in their first state, and so on for types. However, the passage then distinguishes the existent: it cannot be described with contingent actions (moving, sitting, etc.) in its first state, so it also cannot be described with them in non-existence. The author here is presenting these views for later refutation.

Key Terms
الجسم
الموجود
أول أحوال الوجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think al-Khayyat believed that all attributes of existents apply to non-existents, including actions.
Correction: Al-Khayyat explicitly excludes contingent actions like moving and sitting, because they cannot be predicated of an existent at its first moment.

Checkpoint
According to al-Khayyat's principle, which of the following can be attributed to the non-existent?
A) Walking
B) Being a body
C) Eating
D) Sitting

Key Takeaway
Al-Khayyat's principle allows essential categories (body, accident, etc.) to persist in non-existence, but excludes contingent actions.

A Single Proof Against Multiple Claims of Eternity
Al-Nasafi summarizes his refutation of multiple eternity claims with one proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Nasafi refutes multiple claims of eternity (souls, celestial spheres, planets, natures) with a single comprehensive proof of originatedness (ḥudūth).

Core Idea
One proof of origination refutes all claims of eternity.

Imam al-Nasafi has been discussing various groups that claim eternity for different entities. Instead of refuting each group separately, he presents a unified proof that all such things are originated (ḥādith). This passage summarizes that approach, showing the efficiency and power of a well-structured theological argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the list of different claims of eternity mentioned.
The passage is a concluding statement, so it references earlier proofs.
2
Identify the groups and their claims
The passage mentions: (1) Sabians of Harran and al-Razi claim eternity of the rational soul; (2) others claim eternity of celestial spheres and planets; (3) others claim eternity of natures.
Each claim is a distinct doctrine from different philosophical or religious groups.
3
Understand the author's method
Al-Nasafi states he did not engage each group separately but instead established a single proof for the originatedness of all these things. The phrase 'اشتمال ما أقمنا من الدليل على ذلك كله' indicates the proof covers all claims.
This is an example of efficient argumentation in kalām.
4
Reflect on the significance
The lesson shows that a properly constructed proof can refute multiple false doctrines simultaneously, highlighting the coherence of Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تبصرة الأدلة لأبي المعين النسفي ج تعالى وصفاته مادة كان ذلك أم محلا أم زمانا أم نفسا ناطقة كما يدعي قدمها جماعة من صابئة حران وابن زكريا الرازي المتطبب وكذا بطلان قول من يدعي قدم الأفلاك وما فيها من الكواكب أو قدم الطبائع - فلم نشتغل بالكلام مع كل فريق وإثبات حدوث كل شيء من هذه الأشياء لاشتمال ما أقمنا من الدليل على ذلك كله وبالله المعونة والتوفيق.
Tabsirat al-Adilla by Abu al-Mu'in al-Nasafi, volume: Exalted is He and His attributes, whether that be matter, or place, or time, or a rational soul, as a group from the Sabians of Harran and Ibn Zakariya al-Razi the physician claim its eternity. Likewise, the invalidity of the claim of those who claim the eternity of the celestial spheres and what is in them of planets, or the eternity of natures. So we did not engage in argument with every group, but rather established the origination (ḥudūth) of every one of these things, because the proof we have established includes all of that. And with Allah is assistance and success.
Source pages p. 224

Commentary
This passage is a concluding statement from a larger discussion in which Imam al-Nasafi refutes various claims of eternity (qidam) made by different groups. He first identifies the groups: the Sabians of Harran and the physician-philosopher Abu Bakr al-Razi, who claimed the eternity of the rational soul (al-nafs al-nāṭiqa). Then he mentions others who claim the eternity of the celestial spheres and their contents (planets), and those who claim the eternity of natures (al-ṭabāʼiʻ). The author states that instead of engaging in detailed refutations with each group separately, he has presented a single comprehensive proof that demonstrates the originatedness (ḥudūth) of all these things. The phrase 'اشتمال ما أقمنا من الدليل على ذلك كله' indicates that the proof he established covers all these cases, thus efficiently refuting them all. He ends with a prayer for divine assistance and success.

Al-Nasafi's single proof refutes many false claims without wasting effort on each one. Is there a recurring doubt or spiritual struggle you keep trying to fight with piecemeal arguments? The passage mentions that the proof covers even the rational soul—the very thing you rely on to think and reason. Denying an Agent for Generated Actions Undermines the Proof for a Creator
Critique of Mu'tazilite denial of agent for generated actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why denying an agent for generated actions leads to the absurd conclusion that the world could exist without a Creator, thereby invalidating the proof for God's existence.

Core Idea
Denying an agent for generated actions implies the world needs no Creator.

Al-Nasafi critiques Thumama ibn Ashras, a Mu'tazilite leader, for claiming that generated actions have no agent. If that were true, then any temporal event could occur without a maker, ultimately invalidating the proof for God's existence. This passage shows the dangerous theological consequence of such a denial.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to grasp the argument in the author's own words.
The passage is incomplete but the core critique is clear.
2
Identify Thumama's claim and its consequence
Thumama claimed that generated actions have no agent. Since they are temporally originated, he concluded that events can occur without a maker. Notice how Al-Nasafi points out that this invalidates the proof for a Maker.
3
Reflect on why this is self-refuting
If generated actions need no agent, then the entire world could be explained without a Creator, destroying the very proof for God. Consider why this is an absurd position for a theist.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وثمامة بن الأشرس أحد رؤساء المعتزلة أبطل على نفسه دلالة إثبات الصانع فإنه زعم أن المتولدات أفعال لا فاعل لها ولا شك أنها حادثة كائنة بعد أن لم تكن فلما جاز حدوث كائن ما بلا صانع أحدثه جاز في جميع العالم، وكذا أبو الهذيل العلاف وبشر بن المعتمر وابن الراوندي والكرامية يزعمون أن
And Thumama ibn Ashras, one of the leaders of the Mu'tazila, invalidated for himself the proof of the existence of the Maker, for he claimed that generated actions are actions that have no agent, and there is no doubt that they are temporally originated, coming into being after they were not. Since it is permissible for something temporally originated to occur without a maker that brought it into being, then it is permissible for the entire world (to be the same). Likewise, Abu al-Hudhayl al-Allaf, Bishr ibn al-Mu'tamir, Ibn al-Rawandi, and the Karramiyya claim that...
Source pages p. 228

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of certain Mu'tazilite and other groups who deny an agent for 'generated actions' (al-mutawallidat) — events that follow from human actions but are not directly willed, such as the burning caused by fire. Thumama ibn Ashras asserted that these generated actions have no agent at all, and since they are temporally originated, he concluded that it is possible for events to occur without a creator. If such events can occur without a maker, then the entire world could be explained without a creator, thus invalidating the very proof for a Maker. Al-Nasafi points out that this claim is self-refuting. The passage also lists other figures (Abu al-Hudhayl, Bishr, Ibn al-Rawandi, and the Karramiyya) who hold a similar view, though the text cuts off before completing the sentence. The core critique is that denying an agent for generated actions leads to the absurd conclusion that the world could exist without a creator.

Correction: Al-Nasafi shows that if generated actions need no agent, then temporal events can occur without a Creator, undermining the proof for God's existence.

Key Takeaway
Denying an agent for generated actions destroys the proof for a Creator, making the world appear self-sufficient.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
If denying an agent for generated actions leads logically to denying the Creator altogether, how seriously do you take the small choices you make about causality? Will you examine your own assumptions about what 'just happens' and repent from any subtle atheism in your heart?
